باب القياس
٢١٥
حكم القياس
[حكم القياس]
وأما حكمه فتعدية حكم النص إلى ما لا نصّ فيه ليثبت فيه بغالب الرأي على أي فرع مثل حكم النص
أي حكم الأصل
احتمال الخطأ، فالتعدية حكم لازم للتعليل عندنا وعند الشافعي له هو صحيح أي التعليل بدون التعدية، حتى جوّز التعليل بالثمنية، واحتج بأن هذا لما كان من جنس الحجج وجب أن يتعلق به الإيجاب كسائر الحجج.
هذا الفريق على صحتها
لا نص فيه : ولا إجماع ولا دليل فوق الرأي؛ إذ من شروط صحة القياس عدم دليل آخر فوقه. فيه: أي فيما لا فيه وهو الفرع بغالب الرأي إلخ وإنما قال: بغالب" الرأي"؛ لأن القياس من الأدلة الظنية دون القطعية وإن كان وجوب العمل به بطريق اليقين. وفي قوله: "على احتمال" "الخطأ إشارة إلى مذهب منصور ومسلك جمهور، وهو أن كل مجتهد يخطئ ويصيب عندنا: أي عند عامة المتأخرين، وهو قول بعض أصحاب الشافعي وأبي عبد الله البصري من المتكلمين، حتى لو خلا التعليل عن التعدية كان باطلاً، فالقياس والتعليل عند هؤلاء مترادفان. وعند الشافعي له بل عند جمهور الفقهاء والمتكلمين وبعض أصحابنا وأحمد ابن حنبل وأبي الحسن البصري وعبد الجبار والقاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني بدون التعدية: فالتعليل عندهم أعم من القياس والقياس نوع منه؛ لأن التعليل على قسمين، فإن كان العلة فيه متعدّية ثبت الحكم بها في الفرع ويكون قياساً، وإلا فهو تعليل محض أي مجرد عن التعدية، وتسمى العلة علة قاصرة، فإن كانت هي منصدوصة أو مجمعاً عليها فلا خلاف في صحتها عند الفريقين، وإن كانت
مستنبطة كالثمنية في النقدين لحرمة الربا عند الشافعي له فهي عند الفريق الأول غير صحيحة، وعند الثاني صحيحة. بالثمنية: فعند الفريق الثاني علة حرمة بيع الدرهم بالدرهمين الثمنية، وهي مخصوصة في النقدين أي الذهب والفضة، حتى إن ثبت الثمنية في غيرهما لا يحرم البيع بالتفاضل، فهي غير متعدّية بأن هذا أي التعليل بالعلة القاصرة المستنبطة. الحجج: الشرعية التي بها يتعلق الأحكام الشرعية الإيجاب: أي إثبات الأحكام مطلقاً سواء تعدّى إلى الفرع أو لم يتعد. كسائر الحجج الشرعية من الكتاب والسنة، فالحكم يثبت بهما سواء كانا خاصين أو عامين.
مثل: وإنما قدرنا لفظ المثل؛ لأن تعدية عين حكم الأصل غير ثابت؛ لأن الشيء متى تعدى عن محله فرغ محله الأول عنه، وحكم النص يبقى بعد التعليل كما كان، ولا يفرغ.
Chapter of Qiyas. The Ruling of Qiyas. [The Ruling of Qiyas] And as for its ruling, it is the transference of the ruling of the text to what is not mentioned in the text, so that it is established therein by predominant opinion, on any branch like the ruling of the text (i.e., the ruling of the original) with the possibility of error. So transference is a necessary consequence of causation (ta'lil) according to us, while according to al-Shafi'i, causation is valid without transference, to the extent that he permitted causation by the quality of being a medium of exchange (al-thamaniyya). He argued that since this (causation) is one of the types of evidence, it must be that obligation attaches to it like other types of evidence. This group considers it valid. 'No text in it': i.e., no text, nor consensus, nor evidence above opinion; for among the conditions of validity of qiyas is the absence of another evidence above it. 'In it': i.e., in what has no text, which is the branch, by predominant opinion, etc. He only said 'by predominant opinion' because qiyas is among the speculative evidences, not definitive ones, although acting upon it is obligatory with certainty. And in his statement 'with the possibility of error' there is a reference to the view of Mansur and the path of the majority: that every mujtahid may err or be correct. According to us (i.e., generally among the later scholars), and it is the statement of some of the followers of al-Shafi'i and Abu Abdullah al-Basri among the theologians. So if causation is devoid of transference, it is invalid. Thus qiyas and ta'lil are synonymous according to these. And according to al-Shafi'i – rather, according to the majority of jurists and theologians and some of our companions and Ahmad ibn Hanbal and Abu al-Hasan al-Basri and Abd al-Jabbar and al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani – causation is valid without transference. So causation according to them is more general than qiyas, and qiyas is a type of it. Because causation is of two types: if the cause is transmissible, the ruling is established in the branch and it's qiyas; otherwise it is pure causation, i.e., devoid of transference, and the cause is called a qasira (non-transmissible) cause. If the cause is textually specified or agreed by consensus, there is no dispute about its validity among the two groups. But if it is derived (mustanbata), like al-thamaniyya in the two currency metals (gold and silver) for the prohibition of riba according to al-Shafi'i, then according to the first group it is invalid, and according to the second it is valid. By al-thamaniyya: according to the second group, the cause of the prohibition of selling one dirham for two dirhams is the property of being a medium of exchange, and it is specific to the two currency metals (gold and silver). So much so that if thamaniyya is established in other things, selling with disparity is not prohibited. So it is non-transmissible. 'This': i.e., causation with a derived non-transmissible cause. 'Evidences': the shar'i evidences by which shar'i rulings are established. 'Obligation': i.e., establishment of rulings absolutely, whether they transfer to the branch or not, like other shar'i evidences from the Book and Sunnah. So the ruling is established by them whether they are specific or general. 'Like': And we only used the word 'like' (mithl) because transference of the exact same ruling of the original is not established; for when a thing transfers from its place, the original place becomes empty of it, but the ruling of the text remains after causation as it was, and does not become empty.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage defines the ruling (hukm) of qiyas (analogy). The author states that the ruling of qiyas is the transference (ta'diya) of the ruling from the original text to a new case not mentioned in the text. This is done with predominant opinion (ghalib al-ra'y) and involves the possibility of error. Then a key usul dispute is presented: according to the Hanafis, transference is a necessary consequence of causation (ta'lil); a cause that does not transfer (non-transmissible, 'illa qasira) is invalid if it is derived. According to al-Shafi'i and the majority, causation is valid even without transference, as long as the cause is textually specified or agreed upon. They even allow derivation of a non-transmissible cause, such as al-thamaniyya (being a medium of exchange) for the prohibition of riba in gold and silver. The Hanafi position (first group) holds that if the cause does not transfer, the ta'lil is invalid unless it is from text or ijma. The passage also clarifies that 'like' (mithl) is used because the exact same ruling from the original does not literally transfer; the ruling remains in the original and is also established in the branch. This is a fundamental distinction in Hanafi usul: qiyas and ta'lil are not synonymous; ta'lil is broader, and qiyas requires a transmissible cause.

Key Terms
تعليل (ta'lil): Causation: identifying the effective attribute ('illa) that gives rise to a ruling.
تعدية (ta'diya): Transference: the application of the cause from the original to the branch.
علة قاصرة (‘illa qasira): A non-transmissible cause: an attribute that is specific to the original and does not extend to other cases.
ثمنية (thamaniyya): The quality of being a medium of exchange (thaman) as a cause for riba in gold and silver.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is required for a cause ('illa) to be valid in qiyas?
A) The cause must be textually specified.
B) The cause must be transmissible (muta'ddiya) to other cases.
C) The cause must be agreed upon by consensus.
D) The cause must be derived from the original text.

Key Takeaway
For Hanafis, a cause that does not transfer is not a valid basis for analogy; transference is the defining operation of qiyas.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
8 min
The Hanafi Position on Ta'lil with a Non-Transitive Cause
Dispute on whether ta'lil without ta'diya is valid in qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi requirement for transitivity (ta'diya) in ta'lil with derived causes and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using rational proofs.

Core Idea
A derived non-transitive cause is invalid because a Shari' proof must yield knowledge or action—it cannot be futile.

This lesson enters a foundational dispute in Hanafi usul: is cause identification (ta'lil) valid if the cause does not extend to new cases? The Hanafi school insists that ta'lil necessarily involves transference (ta'diya), while Imam al-Shafi'i and many others allow non-transitive causes. We will unpack the rational proof, the counter-arguments, and the practical consequence in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the Hanafi position
Identify the precise question: is ta'lil with a derived non-transitive cause ('illa qasira mustanbata) valid? Note the Hanafi claim that ta'diya is a necessary consequence of ta'lil.
The Hanafi view: if transference is absent, the cause identification is futile and invalid.
2
Analyze the Hanafi rational proof
Examine the argument: a Shari' proof must necessitate either knowledge (ilm) or action (amal). A derived non-transitive cause does neither, so it is futile unless it transfers the ruling.
The proof is aqli (rational); the text provides the full syllogism.
3
Identify the opposing position and its proof
State the Shafi'i view: ta'lil is valid even without transference, because it is a proof like any other (text, consensus) which can be specific. The cause establishes the ruling and restricts it to the original.
The opposing school includes many scholars; they analogize derived causes to textual causes.
4
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal
Understand the Hanafi response: specificity of the ruling is already achieved by not performing ta'lil; also, if a transitive description exists, it must be used. Moreover, a derived cause lacks certainty, unlike a textual one.
The rebuttal dismantles the analogy between derived and textual non-transitive causes.
5
Apply the dispute to a concrete fiqh example
Consider the case of riba in gold and silver. The Shafi'i cause is thamanityya (non-transitive). The Hanafi school rejects this derived non-transitive cause and requires a transitive cause like weight or measure.
This illustrates the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the dispute.

باب القياس
حكم القياس
[حكم القياس]
وأما حكمه فتعدية حكم النص إلى ما لا نصّ فيه ليثبت فيه بغالب الرأي على أي فرع مثل حكم النص أي حكم الأصل احتمال الخطأ، فالتعدية حكم لازم للتعليل عندنا وعند الشافعي له هو صحيح أي التعليل بدون التعدية، حتى جوّز التعليل بالثمنية، واحتج بأن هذا لما كان من جنس الحجج وجب أن يتعلق به الإيجاب كسائر الحجج. هذا الفريق على صحتها لا نص فيه : ولا إجماع ولا دليل فوق الرأي؛ إذ من شروط صحة القياس عدم دليل آخر فوقه. فيه: أي فيما لا فيه وهو الفرع بغالب الرأي إلخ وإنما قال: بغالب" الرأي"؛ لأن القياس من الأدلة الظنية دون القطعية وإن كان وجوب العمل به بطريق اليقين. وفي قوله: "على احتمال" "الخطأ إشارة إلى مذهب منصور ومسلك جمهور، وهو أن كل مجتهد يخطئ ويصيب عندنا: أي عند عامة المتأخرين، وهو قول بعض أصحاب الشافعي وأبي عبد الله البصري من المتكلمين، حتى لو خلا التعليل عن التعدية كان باطلاً، فالقياس والتعليل عند هؤلاء مترادفان. وعند الشافعي له بل عند جمهور الفقهاء والمتكلمين وبعض أصحابنا وأحمد ابن حنبل وأبي الحسن البصري وعبد الجبار والقاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني بدون التعدية: فالتعليل عندهم أعم من القياس والقياس نوع منه؛ لأن التعليل على قسمين، فإن كان العلة فيه متعدّية ثبت الحكم بها في الفرع ويكون قياساً، وإلا فهو تعليل محض أي مجرد عن التعدية، وتسمى العلة علة قاصرة، فإن كانت هي منصدوصة أو مجمعاً عليها فلا خلاف في صحتها عند الفريقين، وإن كانت مستنبطة كالثمنية في النقدين لحرمة الربا عند الشافعي له فهي عند الفريق الأول غير صحيحة، وعند الثاني صحيحة. بالثمنية: فعند الفريق الثاني علة حرمة بيع الدرهم بالدرهمين الثمنية، وهي مخصوصة في النقدين أي الذهب والفضة، حتى إن ثبت الثمنية في غيرهما لا يحرم البيع بالتفاضل، فهي غير متعدّية بأن هذا أي التعليل بالعلة القاصرة المستنبطة. الحجج: الشرعية التي بها يتعلق الأحكام الشرعية الإيجاب: أي إثبات الأحكام مطلقاً سواء تعدّى إلى الفرع أو لم يتعد. كسائر الحجج الشرعية من الكتاب والسنة، فالحكم يثبت بهما سواء كانا خاصين أو عامين. مثل: وإنما قدرنا لفظ المثل؛ لأن تعدية عين حكم الأصل غير ثابت؛ لأن الشيء متى تعدى عن محله فرغ محله الأول عنه، وحكم النص يبقى بعد التعليل كما كان، ولا يفرغ.

باب القياس
حكم القياس
ألا ترى أن دلالة كون الوصف علةً لا يقتضي تعدية بل يعرف ذلك معنى في الوصف. وجه قولنا: أن دليل الشرع لا بد وأنْ يُوجب علماً أو عملاً، وهذا لا علماً يوجب لئلا يكون عبثا التعليل بالعلة القاصرة المستنبطة ولا يوجب عملاً في المنصوص عليه؛ لأنه ثابت بالنص، والنص فوق التعليل، فلا يصح أي في الأصل أي عدول الحكم قطعه عنه، فلم يبق للتعليل حكم سوى التعدية. فإن قيل: التعليل بما لا يتعدى يُفيدُ اختصاص حكم النص به قلنا هذا يحصل بترك التعليل على أن التعليل بما لا يتعدّى التعليل بما يتعدّى، فتبطل هذه الفائدة. لا يمنع ألا ترى أن دلالة إلخ : تائيد على مطلوبه حاصله أن كون الوصف علة للحكم أمر يثبت من التأثير والتعديل وغيره من الأمور، وكونه متعديا أو غير متعد أمر آخر ينشأ من كونه عاماً أو خاصاً، فالتأثير والتعديل وغيره من على كون الوصف علةً للحكم لا يقتضي أن يكون متعدّيا، بل التعدية إنما يعرف من كونه عاما، فإذا دلّت الدلائل على كون الوصف علة للحكم فينبغي أن يحكم على صحته، سواء تعدّى أو لم يتعد؛ لأنه أمر الدلائل الدالة آخر، لا حاجة إليه بعد كون الوصف علة صحيحة لوجود الشرائط. إلى التعليل الذي هو أضعف من علما أي يقيناً لكونه دليلا ظنّيا بالاتفاق فوق التعليل: لأنه قطعي، فأي حاجة إلى أن يضاف حكم الأصل النص مع وجود النص فيه عنه: أي عن النص وإيجابه إلى التعليل. . سوى التعدية: فلو خلا عنها أيضا كما خلا عن العلم كان عبثاً وباطلاً، وأما العلة القاصرة المنصوصة فهى ليست على هذا الدَيْدَن؛ لأنها مفيدة للعلم؛ إذ الشارع لما نص عليها فقد أفاد علماً بأنها هي المؤثرة في الحكم، ولا فائدة أعظم منها. فإن قيل: منع على قوله: فلم يبق للتعليل حكم سوى التعدية، حاصله أنا لانسلّم الانحصار في هاتين الفائدتين، بل يجوز أن يكون له فائدة أخرى وهي إثبات اختصاص الحكم بالنص لئلا يشتغل المجتهد بالتعليل للتعدية إلى الفرع بعد ما عرف اختصاص الحكم به. بترك التعليل: لأن هذا الاختصاص كان ثابتاً قبل التعليل يفوت العموم الحاصل به، فبقي الخصوص على حاله. لا يمنع التعليل بما يتعدّى: لأنه كما جاز أن يجتمع في الأصل وصفان ،متعديان، أحدهما أكثر تعدياً من الآخر كذلك يجوز أن يجتمع فيه ،وصفان أحدهما يتعدّى والآخر لا يتعدّى، فإذا علّل المجتهد بوصف غير متعد لا يحصل الاختصاص به؛ لأن الوصف المتعدّي موجود فيه، فيجب عليه أن يعلّل بوصف متعدّ؛ لأنه أقرب إلى الاعتبار المأمور به من غير المتعدّي، =
Chapter of Analogy.
Ruling of Analogy.
As for its ruling, it is the transference of the ruling of a text to that for which there is no text, so that it is established therein by predominant opinion upon any branch similar to the ruling of the text, i.e., the ruling of the original, with the possibility of error. So transference is a necessary consequence of cause identification (ta'lil) according to us, whereas according to al-Shafi'i, cause identification without transference is valid, such that he permitted cause identification by thamanityya (being a medium of exchange). He argued that since this is of the kind of proofs, it must be that obligation attaches to it like all other proofs. This group holds its validity. [The text says] 'for which there is no text': i.e., neither a text, nor consensus, nor a proof above opinion; because among the conditions for the validity of analogy is the absence of another proof above it. 'In it': i.e., in what has no text, which is the branch, 'by predominant opinion' etc. He only said 'by predominant opinion' because analogy is from the probable proofs, not certain ones, even though acting upon it is obligatory with certainty. And in his statement 'upon the possibility of error' is a reference to the school of Mansur and the way of the majority, which is that every mujtahid is either in error or correct. According to us: i.e., according to the later scholars, and it is the saying of some of al-Shafi'i's companions and Abu 'Abd Allah al-Basri among the theologians, such that if cause identification is devoid of transference, it is invalid. So analogy and cause identification are synonymous according to these. According to al-Shafi'i, rather according to the majority of jurists and theologians and some of our companions and Ahmad ibn Hanbal and Abu al-Hasan al-Basri and 'Abd al-Jabbar and al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, cause identification without transference is valid. Thus cause identification according to them is more general than analogy, and analogy is a type of it; because cause identification is of two types: if the cause in it is transitive, the ruling is established thereby in the branch and it is analogy; otherwise it is pure cause identification, i.e., devoid of transference, and the cause is called a non-transitive cause (illa qasira). If it is a textual cause or agreed upon, there is no disagreement about its validity according to the two groups. But if it is derived, like thamanityya in the two precious metals (gold and silver) for the prohibition of riba according to al-Shafi'i, then according to the first group it is invalid, and according to the second it is valid. 'By thamanityya': according to the second group, the cause for the prohibition of selling a dirham for two dirhams is thamanityya, which is specific to the two precious metals, i.e., gold and silver, such that if thamanityya is established in other than them, selling with disparity is not prohibited. So it is non-transitive. That is, cause identification with a derived non-transitive cause. 'Proofs': the Shari proofs by which Shari rulings are attached. 'Obligation': i.e., establishing rulings absolutely, whether they transfer to the branch or not, like all other Shari proofs from the Book and Sunnah, so the ruling is established by them whether they are specific or general. An example: we only bring the word 'like' because the transference of the very ruling of the original is not established; because when something transfers from its place, its first place becomes empty of it. But the ruling of the text remains after cause identification as it was, and does not become empty.

Chapter of Analogy.
Ruling of Analogy.
Do you not see that the indication that a description is a cause does not necessitate transference, rather that is known by the meaning in the description? The point of our statement is that a Shari proof must necessitate knowledge or action. This does not necessitate knowledge, lest it be futile (the cause identification with a derived non-transitive cause) and does not necessitate action regarding the original text because it is established by the text, and the text is above cause identification. So it is not valid (in the original). That is, because the removal of the ruling from it has cut it off, so there remains for cause identification no effect except transference. If it is said: 'Cause identification with what does not transfer bestows specificity of the ruling of the text to it', we say: 'This is obtained by abandoning cause identification, and cause identification with what does not transfer is like cause identification with what transfers, so this benefit is nullified.' It does not prevent that you see that indication etc. is a support for his claim. The upshot is that a description being a cause for a ruling is established by influence, appropriateness, and other matters, and its being transitive or non-transitive is another matter arising from its being general or specific. So influence, appropriateness, and other indications of a description being a cause for a ruling do not require it to be transitive; rather transference is only known from its being general. So if the proofs indicate that the description is a cause for the ruling, then it should be judged valid whether it is transitive or not, because it is another matter, not needed after the description is a valid cause due to the presence of conditions. To cause identification which is weaker than knowledge, i.e., certainty, because it is a probable proof by agreement, but the text is above cause identification because it is certain. So what need is there to attribute the ruling of the original text, with the existence of the text therein, to cause identification? Except transference: if it were devoid of it as it is devoid of knowledge, it would be futile and invalid. As for the non-transitive cause that is textual, it is not on this norm because it provides knowledge; since the Lawgiver when He stated it has provided knowledge that it is the effective cause of the ruling, and there is no benefit greater than that. If it is said: 'Objection to his statement: there remains for cause identification no effect except transference.' The gist is that we do not concede the restriction to these two benefits; rather it may have another benefit, namely establishing the specificity of the ruling to the text, so that the mujtahid does not engage in cause identification for transference to the branch after having known the specificity of the ruling to it. 'By abandoning cause identification': because this specificity was established before cause identification, but the generality that occurs by it is lost, so the specificity remains as it was. 'It does not prevent cause identification with what transfers': because just as it is possible that in the original there are two transitive descriptions, one more transitive than the other, similarly it is possible that there are two descriptions, one transitive and the other non-transitive. So if the mujtahid performs cause identification with a non-transitive description, specificity is not obtained by it because the transitive description exists in it. Therefore he must perform cause identification with a transitive description because it is closer to the consideration commanded than the non-transitive. =
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Qiyas (analogy) in a Hanafi usul work discusses the ruling (hukm) of qiyas and a key dispute regarding the validity of ta'lil (cause identification) with a non-transitive cause ('illa qasira). The text begins by defining qiyas as the transference of a ruling from a text (asl) to a new case (far') that has no textual ruling, based on predominant opinion with the possibility of error. It then states the Hanafi position that transference (ta'diya) is a necessary consequence of ta'lil; without it, ta'lil is invalid if the cause is derived (mustanbata). In contrast, Imam al-Shafi'i and a majority of jurists and theologians hold that ta'lil without transference is valid. The dispute centers on whether a derived non-transitive cause (like thamanityya in gold and silver for riba) can be a valid legal cause. The Hanafi argument is that a Shari' proof must yield either knowledge or action. A derived non-transitive cause yields neither: it does not yield knowledge because it is conjectural (zanni), and it does not yield action in the original because the text already establishes the ruling. Hence it is futile. The only effect such ta'lil can have is transference. The text also discusses a counterargument that non-transitive ta'lil might serve to restrict the ruling to the original, but the Hanafi reply is that such restriction is already achieved by leaving it un-explained. The passage concludes by noting that if a transitive description exists alongside a non-transitive one, the mujtahid must use the transitive one as it better serves the commanded i'tibar (consideration).

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Transference of the ruling of a text (asl) to a new case (far') that has no textual ruling, based on a shared cause, by predominant opinion with possibility of error.
التعليل (al-ta'lil): Identification of the legal cause ('illa) that necessitates a ruling. In this passage, it is distinguished from qiyas: ta'lil can be with or without transference.
العلة القاصرة (al-'illa al-qasira): A cause that is non-transitive, i.e., it does not extend to other cases beyond the original. In this context, it is a cause confined to the original text.
المستنبطة (al-mustanbata): A derived cause, i.e., one extracted by ijtihad from the text, as opposed to a textual cause explicitly stated by the Lawgiver.
المنصوصة (al-mansusa): A textual cause, i.e., one explicitly mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah as the effective cause.
التعدية (al-ta'diya): Transitivity of the cause; the ability of the cause to extend the ruling from the original (asl) to a new case (far').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi school's main argument against the validity of a derived non-transitive cause?
A) Such a cause is not mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah.
B) It does not produce certain knowledge nor a new obligatory action, making it futile.
C) It is rejected because it contradicts consensus.
D) It is valid only if the original text is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires that a derived cause in qiyas be transitive (ta'diya) to be valid; a derived non-transitive cause is futile because it yields no new knowledge or action.

When an Intransitive Cause Is Invalid in Hanafi Qiyās
Argument that non-transitive cause is invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an intransitive cause (al-'illah al-qāṣirah) is valid or invalid in Hanafi qiyās and apply the rule that a derived intransitive cause is invalid unless textually indicated.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, an intransitive cause derived by ijtihad is invalid — only a transitive cause yields the required knowledge or action.

This passage tackles a key debate in Hanafi usul: can a cause that does not extend to new cases (non-transitive) still be used in qiyās? The author argues that a derived intransitive cause is futile because it neither produces certainty (since it is conjectural) nor compels action (since the original ruling is already textually established). However, a textually indicated intransitive cause is valid because it provides knowledge. This distinction is essential for correctly constructing qiyās.

Guided Path
1
State the rule and the two types of intransitive causes
Identify the author's claim: a derived intransitive cause (al-'illah al-qāṣirah al-mustanbiṭah) is invalid, but a textually indicated intransitive cause (al-manṣūṣah) is valid. Understand the reason: the former yields neither knowledge nor action, while the latter provides certain knowledge.
2
Analyse the rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī)
The author states: 'The legal proof must necessitate knowledge or action. This [non-transitive cause] does not necessitate knowledge, lest it be futile, nor does it necessitate action in the textually established [original] because the text is above the explanation.' Break down each part: knowledge (ʿilm) is not produced because the cause is conjectural (ẓannī); action (ʿamal) is not produced because the ruling is already binding by the text itself.
3
Examine the objection and rebuttal
An objector says the intransitive cause benefits by specifying the ruling exclusively to the original case. The author rebuts: (1) specification is achieved even without the cause; (2) an intransitive cause in the original implies the presence of a transitive cause, so the intended specification fails. The mujtahid must use the transitive cause whenever possible.
4
Distinguish textually indicated causes
The author clarifies that the ruling does not apply to intransitive causes that are textually indicated (manṣūṣah). Because the Legislator has explicitly designated the cause, it provides certain knowledge (ʿilm). Such a cause is valid even if it does not extend to branches.
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Given a concrete case, decide whether a proposed cause is valid or invalid based on the principles above. For example, if the cause is derived by ijtihad and does not extend to other cases, it is invalid. If the cause is explicitly mentioned in the Qur'an or hadith, it is valid even if intransitive.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of qiyās, specifically addressing the validity of causes that are transitive (muta'ddiyah) versus intransitive (qāṣirah). The author argues that an intransitive cause derived by ijtihad is invalid because it neither produces knowledge nor action, except for the purpose of extending the ruling to a branch. If it does not extend, it is futile. However, an intransitive cause that is textually indicated (manṣūṣah) is valid because it provides knowledge. The author then addresses an objection: that intransitive causes might provide the benefit of specifying the ruling exclusively to the text. He rebuts that this specification is achieved without the cause, and further, that an intransitive cause implies a transitive cause in the same original, so the intended specification fails. The correct method is to use a transitive cause whenever possible. This is a Hanafi position emphasizing that qiyās requires a transitive 'illah that can apply to branches.

Key Terms
العلة القاصرة (al-'illah al-qāṣirah): An intransitive cause that does not extend the ruling to a branch
العلة المتعدية (al-'illah al-muta'ddiyah): A transitive cause that extends the ruling from the original to a branch
العلة المنصوصة (al-'illah al-manṣūṣah): A cause that is explicitly stated in the text

Types of 'Illah and the Four Modes of Refuting Formal Causes
Classification of causes and the four modes of refuting formal causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between formal and effective causes and apply the four modes of refuting formal causes in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Hanafis reject formal causes that rely on mere co-existence; they refute them through four defined modes to force acceptance of effective causes.

In qiyas, identifying the correct cause is essential. The Hanafi school distinguishes between formal causes (which merely accompany the ruling) and effective causes (whose effect is proven by text or consensus). This lesson explores that distinction and presents the four methods used to refute a formal cause, a foundational skill for usul debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of causes
Notice that the passage begins by classifying causes into formal (ṭardiyyah) and effective (mu'aththirah). Understand that this classification is the basis for the entire refutation discussion.
Formal causes are rejected by Hanafis; effective causes are accepted.
2
Define formal cause and effective cause
Extract the definitions: formal cause is a description where the ruling merely coexists without proof of effect; effective cause is one whose effect is proven by text or consensus in the genus of the ruling.
The example of the cat's circumambulation illustrates an effective cause.
3
Understand the Hanafi position and the Shafi'i opposing view
Hanafis require effective causes; Shafi'is accept formal causes. The passage states that the Hanafi refutation forces Shafi'is to accept effectiveness.
This ikhtilaf is central to many qiyas debates.
4
List and explain the four modes of refuting formal causes
Memorize the four modes: acceptance of necessary implication (qawl bi mūjib), objection (mumāna'ah), invalidity of basis (fasād al-waḍ'), contradiction (munāqaḍah). Understand the order of precedence given in the passage.
Each mode targets a different weakness in the opponent's argument.
5
Apply the first mode (qawl bi mūjib) with the fasting example
The example: Shafi'is claim obligation of fasting requires specific intention each night. The Hanafi refutation accepts that implication but then shows it leads to an unacceptable consequence or inconsistency.
This mode is given precedence because it cuts off the argument directly.

Chapter of Qiyās: Refutation of causes. The causes, objection, and contradiction. As for its refutation, we say: The causes are of two types: formal (ṭardiyyah) and effective (mu'aththirah). Each of the two categories has subdivisions. Meaning the refutation of the opposing qiyās. Meaning the formal and effective. From the refutation. The formal causes are four. As for the modes of refuting formal causes, they are four: acceptance of the necessary implication of the cause (al-qawl bi mūjib al-'illah), then objection (al-mumāna'ah), then showing the invalidity of the basis (bayān fasād al-waḍ'), then contradiction (al-munāqaḍah). As for acceptance of the necessary implication, it is accepting what follows from the opponent's cause. For example, their statement regarding the fast of Ramadan: it is an obligatory fast, so it is not performed except with specific intention. Meaning the statement of the Shāfi'īs. When this possibility arose in everything you have established specification by a non-transitive description, the specification is invalid. This benefit you have claimed, namely specification of the ruling to the texts. And when he finished with the ruling, he began to explain its refutation. Formal: The formal cause is a description in which the co-existence of the ruling with it is considered, existing according to some and both existing and lacking according to others, without regard to its effect being established in a place by text or consensus. Arguing by it is not valid according to us, though the Shāfi'īs argue by it. We [argue] by the effective cause, and we refute formal causes in a way that forces the Shāfi'īs to accept effectiveness. And the Shāfi'īs refute the effective cause, then we answer their refutation. This discussion is the foundation of debate; the science of debate was derived from this uṣūlī discussion with some rules changed and added. And effective: The effective cause is that whose effect appears by text or consensus in the genus of the ruling explained by it, like the cat's circumambulation, which is proven to be a cause for lifting the ruling of impurity in its leftover water by a sound hadith, as its explanation has passed, so remember it. Subdivisions of refutation: as you will encounter now, God willing. Acceptance of the necessary implication: meaning accepting what follows from the opponent's explanation; for this reason it is given precedence over others, because it cuts off the argument. Objection: its dispute is less compared to those that follow, so it is given precedence over others. Invalidity of the basis: and since this is a stronger refutation than contradiction, it is given precedence over it. What follows from the opponent's explanation: with the dispute in the contested ruling remaining, except for specific intention, such as saying: 'I intended tomorrow's fast for the obligatory Ramadan' by intending each day. And the Shāfi'īs have established this ruling by the formal cause, which is obligation (farḍiyyah) for specification; for whenever obligation exists, specification exists, as you see in the fast of make-up and expiation.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage introduces the refutation of causes (daf' al-'ilal) in qiyās. The commentator begins by classifying causes into two types: formal (ṭardiyyah) and effective (mu'aththirah). Formal causes are those where the ruling merely coexists with the description without any proof of effect; effective causes are those whose effect is proven by text or consensus. The Hanafis reject formal causes and require effective causes, while the Shāfi'īs use formal causes. The passage then lists the four modes of refuting formal causes: (1) acceptance of the necessary implication (al-qawl bi mūjib al-'illah) — agreeing that the opponent's cause leads to a certain consequence, which may be problematic; (2) objection (al-mumāna'ah) — denying the existence of the cause; (3) showing the invalidity of the basis (bayān fasād al-waḍ') — demonstrating that the cause is not valid in its own right; (4) contradiction (al-munāqaḍah) — pointing out inconsistency. The first mode is elaborated with an example from the Shāfi'īs regarding fasting in Ramadan requiring specific intention at night daily. The Shāfi'īs use the formal cause of obligation (farḍiyyah) to prove this requirement. The Hanafi refutation forces them to admit that only effective causes can prove such a ruling.

Key Terms
العلة الطردية (al-'illah al-ṭardiyyah): A description in which the ruling is only observed to coexist, without proof of effectiveness by text or consensus
العلة المؤثرة (al-'illah al-mu'aththirah): A cause whose effect is proven by text or consensus in the genus of the explained ruling
القول بموجب العلة (al-qawl bi mūjib al-'illah): Accepting the necessary implication of the opponent's cause, often to expose a problematic consequence
الممانعة (al-mumāna'ah): Objecting to the existence of the claimed cause in the original or branch
بيان فساد الوضع (bayān fasād al-waḍ'): Showing that the claimed cause is invalid in its basis, e.g., incorrect or unfounded
المناقضة (al-munāqaḍah): Contradiction: showing that the opponent's cause leads to a contradiction in their own system

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of a formal cause (al-'illah al-ṭardiyyah)?
A) A cause whose effect is proven by text or consensus.
B) A description where the ruling coexists without proof of effect.
C) A cause that is always transitive.
D) A cause that is mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The four modes of refuting formal causes are essential Hanafi tools that force opponents to rely on textually proven effective causes, ensuring qiyas is grounded in evidentiary support.

The Four Types of Objection (Mumana'a) in Qiyas
Enumeration and explanation of four types of objection in analogical reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four types of muman'a in qiyas and apply them to a Hanafi usul argument.

Core Idea
In Hanafi qiyas, an opponent blocks the analogy by challenging the description, its suitability, the ruling, or the relation.

In dialectical reasoning (munazarah), the opponent (sa'il) does not simply deny the conclusion; they must raise a formal objection (muman'a) against the premises of the qiyas. The Hanafi usul tradition classifies these objections into four precise types, each targeting a different component of the analogy. Understanding these types sharpens your ability to both construct sound analogies and refute weak ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of a qiyas argument
Recall that a qiyas has four pillars: the original case (asl), the new case (far'), the effective cause ('illah), and the ruling (hukm).
The 'illah is the description that connects the ruling from the asl to the far'.
2
Define muman'a and its subtypes
Muman'a is an objection that denies one of the premises. The text lists four: against the existence of the description, its suitability as 'illah, the correctness of the claimed ruling, or the causal relation.
These are distinct from mu'araḍa (counter-evidence).
3
Work through the example for each type
For each type, study the provided example from the Hanafi-Shafi'i debate. Identify which premise is being challenged and how the Hanafi responds.
The triplication of wiping the head example involves all three types except the fourth.
4
Apply the classification to a new scenario
Given a short qiyas argument, determine which type of muman'a an opponent would raise and write the objection.
Focus on the logical structure, not the fiqh ruling.

Chapter of Qiyas: The Causes, Objection, and Contradiction. It is said to them: According to us, it is not valid except with the specification of intention, but we permit it with the absolute intention on the basis that it is specification. Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage discusses the four types of muman'a (objection) in qiyas as used by the Hanafi school. Objections are raised by the opponent (sa'il) against the proponent (mustadill) to block the analogy. The first type denies the existence of the description in the original case. The second denies its suitability as a cause ('illah). The third denies that the claimed ruling is correct, asserting a different ruling. The fourth denies the attribution of the ruling to that description. The text provides examples: for the first type, the analogy between wiping the head and istinja is rejected because the description of 'wiping' differs (istinja removes actual impurity). For the second, the Shafi'i cause of 'virginity' for guardianship is rejected; the Hanafi maintain that minority is the suitable cause. For the third, regarding triplication of wiping, the Hanafi argue that the ruling is not triplication but completion (istikmal), which varies. The debate on the specification of intention for fasting is also addressed: the opponent claims that obligation necessarily entails specification, but the Hanafi distinguish between specification by the servant and by the Lawgiver; the latter suffices here.

Key Terms
ممانعة (mumāna‘a): An objection raised by the opponent in a qiyas argument, challenging one of the premises.
علة (‘illa): The effective cause that connects the ruling to the description.
تثليث (tathlīth): Doing something three times; triplication.
استيعاب (istī‘āb): Complete coverage; covering the entire part.
تعين النية (ta‘ayyun al-niyya): Specification of intention; designating a particular act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of muman'a involves the opponent denying that the description claimed by the proponent is suitable to be the cause ('illah)?
A) Muman'a in the description itself
B) Muman'a in its suitability for the ruling
C) Muman'a in the ruling itself
D) Muman'a in the relation of the ruling to the description

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of muman'a into four types provides a precise tool to analyze and construct objections in qiyas, ensuring arguments are logically sound.

Fasad al-Wad' (Invalidity of Basis) as a Qiyas Objection
Definition of fasad al-wad' with two marriage examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define fasad al-wad', apply it to identify when a qiyas argument is invalid, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i position on apostasy's effect on marriage.

Core Idea
A cause that contradicts the ruling by text or consensus is fasad al-wad' — the analogy collapses.

In this lesson, we examine a powerful objection in Hanafi usul: fasad al-wad'. This occurs when the description alleged as a cause is, by text or consensus, established as a cause for the opposite ruling. We will see how Hanafis use this to refute a Shafi'i analogy regarding the effect of apostasy on marriage. Understanding this objection sharpens your ability to evaluate qiyas arguments critically.

Guided Path
1
Define fasad al-wad'
Identify the formal definition: a description that is inherently contradictory to the ruling and is proven by text or consensus to be a cause for the opposite ruling. This forces the proponent to abandon pure attribute-based analogy (tard) and seek evidence of appropriateness (mulā'ama) and effectiveness (ta'thīr).
This is a strong objection because it attacks the very basis of the claimed cause.
2
Analyze the first example: Islam of one spouse
Examine the claim that Islam of one spouse causes separation. The Hanafi response: Islam is known to preserve rights, not negate them; thus, making it a cause for separation is fasad al-wad'. The separation should be attributed to the other spouse's refusal to embrace Islam.
Shafi'is said immediate separation if unconsummated, after 'idda if consummated; but Hanafis attribute separation to rejection of Islam.
3
Analyze the second example: apostasy of one spouse
Shafi'is argue that apostasy delays separation until the end of 'idda for a consummated marriage, analogizing to divorce. Hanafis argue this is fasad al-wad' because apostasy contradicts the very basis of marriage (protection of life and property), so it cannot be a cause for preserving the marriage even temporarily.
Apostasy invalidates 'isma (protection), so the cause (apostasy) must produce its effect (separation) immediately.
4
Apply the objection to the Shafi'i analogy
Show that the Shafi'i qiyas is invalid because the description (apostasy) is textually and consensually a cause for separation, not for preservation. This demonstrates fasad al-wad' and forces the proponent to abandon tard and seek mulā'ama.
The Hanafi position is immediate separation, which follows logically from the correct cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فساد الوضع وهو كون الوصف في نفسه آبياً عن الحكم ومقتضياً لضده بأن يثبت بنص أو إجماع كونه علة لنقيض هذا الحكم، فإذا أورد على المستدلّ هذا السوال يضطر إلى الرجوع عن الطرد إلى بيان الملائمة والتأثير في القياس. بإسلام أحد الزوجين فإنهم قالوا: إذا أسلم أحد الزوجين، فإن كانت المرأة غير مدخول بها تقع الفرقة بمجرد الإسلام من غير توقف على قضاء القاضي، وانقضاء العدة كردّة أحدهما، وإن كانت مدخول بها فبعد مضي ثلاث حيض، فقد جعلوا الإسلام علة الفرقة ونحن نقول: هذا في وضعه فاسد، فإن الإسلام لا يصلح أن يكون علة الفرقة؛ لأنه عُرف عاصماً للحقوق لا رافعاً لها، فينبغي أن يعرض الإسلام على الآخر، فإن أسلم بقي النكاح بينهما كما كان، وإلا تضاف الفرقة إلى الإباء لا إلى الإسلام والاباء يصلح أن يكون علة الفرقة. ولإبقاء إلخ عطف على "لإيجاب" "الفرقة" أي ومثل تعليلهم لإبقاء النكاح إلخ، إذا ارتدّ أحد الزوجين – والعياذ بالله العظيم – فإن كانت المرأة غير مدخول بها تقع الفرقة في الحال اتفاقاً، وإن كانت مدخولاً بها فكذا عندنا خلافاً للشافعية، فعندهم لاتقع الفرقة حتى تنقضي عدتها، فتعليلهم لإبقاء النكاح وقت ارتداد أحدهما إلى انقضاء العدة بأن هذه فرقة وجبت بسبب طارئ على النكاح غير مناف إياه وهو الردة، فوجب أن يتأجل إلى انقضاء العدة في المدخول بها لطلاق فاسد في وضعه؛ لأنه تعليل لإبقاء الشيء مع ما ينافيه وهو الارتداد، فإنه مناف للنكاح؛ لأنه يبطل عصمة النفس والمال جميعاً، والنكاح مبني على العصمة، ولما كان الحكم يضاف إلى الحادث أبداً أو إلى آخر الأوصاف وجوداً، وفيما نحن فيه الردة حادث أنسبنا الفرقة إلى الردة، فإذا ثبت العلة ثبت المعلول، وهو الفرقة من غير توقف إلى انقضاء العدة، ولا يذهب إلى الفهم أن الشافعية يجعلون الارتداد علة =
Invalidity of basis: it is that the description is inherently contradictory to the ruling and necessitates its opposite, established by text or consensus as a cause for the opposite of this ruling. So when this question is posed to the proponent, he is forced to retreat from pure analogy (tard) to demonstrating appropriateness and effectiveness in the qiyas. Regarding the Islam of one spouse: they said if one spouse becomes Muslim... (full translation omitted for brevity)

Commentary
This passage defines and exemplifies fasad al-wad' (invalidity of basis), a strong objection in qiyas. The description claimed as a cause is, by text or consensus, established as a cause for the opposite ruling. The example: Shafi'is hold that Islam of one spouse causes separation (firqa) immediately if the marriage is unconsummated, or after three menses if consummated. The Hanafi argue this is fasad al-wad' because Islam is known to preserve rights, not negate them; it is contradictory to make it a cause for separation. Instead, the separation should be attributed to the other spouse's refusal to embrace Islam. The second example: apostasy of one spouse. Hanafis hold that separation occurs immediately even if the marriage is consummated, while Shafi'is delay until end of 'idda. The Hanafi argue that the Shafi'i reasoning (preserving the marriage despite apostasy) is fasad al-wad' because apostasy contradicts marriage (it violates 'isma of life and property), so the cause (apostasy) must produce its effect (separation) immediately.

Key Terms
فساد الوضع (fasād al-waḍ‘): A description that is inherently contradictory to the ruling, established by text or consensus as a cause for the opposite ruling.
طرد (ṭard): Pure analogical reasoning based solely on the presence of a description without further justification.

Key Takeaway
Fasad al-wad' is a powerful objection that invalidates a qiyas argument when the claimed cause is textually proven to be a cause for the opposite ruling.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Effective Causes and Their Immunity to Munāqaḍah
Explains the immunity of effective causes and methods to defend them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an effective cause, state that it is immune to munāqaḍah and fasād al-waḍ‘, list the four defense methods for imagined contradictions, and apply the rule to the provided examples.

Core Idea
An effective cause proven by a definitive textual source cannot be challenged by contradiction or invalidity, only by counter-evidence.

In Hanafi usul, not all legal causes are equal. Once a cause is established by the Quran, Sunnah, or Ijma, it attains a special status: it becomes immune to common objections like contradiction or structural invalidity. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this immunity and equips you with the tools to defend an effective cause against formal objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
Recognize that the passage is about 'effective causes' (al-‘ilal al-mu’aththirah) – those whose legal effect has been proven by a definitive source.
The Arabic phrase 'العلة المؤثرة' literally means 'effective cause'.
2
Understand the immunity
Learn that these causes do not accept munāqaḍah (contradiction) or fasād al-waḍ‘ (invalidity of the cause) after their effect is manifested.
Immunity is not absolute for all causes; only for those proven by Book, Sunnah, or Ijma.
3
Recognize the only valid objection
Know that the only objection allowed is mu‘āraḍah – presenting a counter-cause that conflicts with the original one.
Mu‘āraḍah is a different form of argument, not a direct attack on the cause's validity.
4
Learn the four defense methods
If a contradiction is imagined, the Hanafi usuli repels it using four methods in order: daf‘ bi al-waṣf (by specifying the description), bi al-badal (by substituting the cause), bi al-jawāb (by giving a direct answer), and bi al-man‘ (by preventing the objection from being raised).
These methods protect the effective cause even when an apparent contradiction appears.
5
Apply to the examples
Study the three examples: Quran: exit from body (cause for impurity); Sunnah: circumambulation (cause for purity of cat's leftover water); Ijma: causing loss (cause for not cutting hand on third theft).
Each example shows that once the cause's effect is proven, it cannot be overturned by simple inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العلل المؤثرة فليس للسائل فيها بعد الممانعة إلا المعارضة؛ لأنها لا تحتمل المناقضة، وفساد الوضع بعد ما ظهر أثرها بالكتاب أو السنة أو الإجماع، لكنه إذا تصوّر مناقضة يجب دفعه من وجوه أربعة: الدفع بالوصف ثم بالبدل ثم بالجواب ثم بالمنع. مثال ما ظهر أثره بالكتاب كالخارج من البدن، فإنه علة للحدث، فظهر تأثيره في الحدث مرة في السبيلين بقوله تعالى: ﴿أَوْ جَاءَ أَحَدٌ مِنْكُمْ مِنَ الْغَائِطِ﴾ (النساء: 43)، ومثال ما ظهر تأثيره بالسنة كالطواف، فإنه علة لعدم نجاسة سؤر سواكن البيوت، فإنه ظهر تأثيره مرةً في سؤر الهرة لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: 'إنما هي من الطوافين عليكم والطوافات' [الترمذي رقم: 92]، ومثال ما ظهر تأثيره بالإجماع كالإتلاف، فإنه علة لعدم قطع يد السارق في المرة الثالثة، فإن فيه تفويت جنس المنفعة على الكمال، وحد السرقة إنما شرع زاجراً لا مُتلفاً بالإجماع، فلو قطع يده في المرة الثالثة لزم الإتلاف الممنوع إجماعاً، فتلك العلل لا تحتمل فساد الوضع أصلا بأن لا تصلح علة؛ لأنه ظهرت عليتها مرةً من الكتاب والسنة والإجماع، وأما المناقضة فإنها تتجه عليها صورة وإن لم تتجه حقيقة. وإليه أشار بقوله: لكنه إذا تصوّر إلخ: وهي الدفع بالوصف ثم
As for effective causes (al-‘ilal al-mu’aththirah), the questioner after the prevention (mumāna‘ah) has only counter-evidence (mu‘āraḍah) because they do not admit contradiction (munāqaḍah) or invalidity of position (fasād al-waḍ‘) after their effect has been manifested by the Book, the Sunnah, or consensus. However, if a contradiction is imagined, it must be repelled in four ways: repelling by description, then by substitution, then by response, then by prevention. An example of what manifested its effect by the Book: the exit from the body, for it is a cause for ritual impurity, and its effect appeared in impurity once in the two passages by His saying: 'Or one of you comes from the toilet' (al-Nisa 43). An example of what manifested its effect by the Sunnah: circumambulation (ṭawāf), for it is a cause for the non-impurity of the leftover water of domestic animals, for its effect appeared once in the leftover water of the cat due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'It is among those who go around you and go around' (al-Tirmidhi no. 92). An example of what manifested its effect by consensus: causing loss (itlāf), for it is a cause for not cutting the hand of a thief in the third instance, for it involves the forfeiture of the entire benefit, and the punishment of theft was prescribed as a deterrent, not as a destroyer by consensus. So if his hand were cut in the third instance, it would entail the destruction that is prohibited by consensus. Thus, these causes absolutely do not admit invalidity of position, because their causality has been manifested once by the Book, Sunnah, or consensus. As for contradiction, it faces them in form but not in reality. And he alluded to this by his saying: 'However, if a contradiction is imagined, etc.' And it is the repelling by description, then...
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage explains the special status of effective causes (al-‘ilal al-mu’aththirah) in Hanafi usul. After a cause has been proven to produce its legal effect by a definitive source (Quran, mutawatir sunnah, or ijma), it cannot be challenged by claiming that the cause is invalid (fasād al-waḍ‘) or that it fails to produce its effect in some case (munāqaḍah). The only valid objection is mu‘āraḍah (counter-evidence), i.e., proposing a different cause that conflicts. However, if a contradiction appears in form (sūrah), the Hanafi scholar must repel it using four methods: (1) al-daf‘ bi al-waṣf (repelling by describing the cause more precisely), (2) bi al-badal (by substituting the cause), (3) bi al-jawāb (by answering), (4) bi al-man‘ (by preventing the objection). The passage gives three examples: the exit from the body (e.g., urine, stool) as a cause for ritual impurity from Quran; circumambulation as a cause for the purity of leftover water of cats from sunnah; and causing loss (itlāf) as a cause for not cutting the thief’s hand on the third theft from ijma. These examples show that once the cause’s effect is established by a decisive proof, the cause cannot be overturned by simple inconsistency.

Key Terms
العلة المؤثرة (al-‘illah al-mu’aththirah): A legal cause whose effect has been proven by a definitive textual source (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma).
المعارضة (al-mu‘āraḍah): A counter-argument presenting an alternative cause that conflicts with the original cause.
فساد الوضع (fasād al-waḍ‘): Claiming that a putative cause is invalid as a legal cause because it cannot produce the effect in principle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following objections is impermissible against an effective cause?
A) Mu‘āraḍah (counter-evidence).
B) Munāqaḍah (contradiction).
C) Fasād al-waḍ‘ (invalidity of the cause).
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Effective causes established by definitive textual sources are legally immune to objections of contradiction and invalidity; only counter-evidence (mu‘āraḍah) is admissible.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate

The Immunity of Effective Causes from Inconsistency
Explains the immunity of effective causes and the permissible objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an effective cause (al-‘illah al-mu’aththira) and explain why it cannot be invalidated by naqd or fasd al-wad‘, and state the only permissible objection (mu‘arada).

Core Idea
An effective cause proven by Quran, Sunnah, or Ijma is immune to inconsistency and invalidity of basis; only counter-analogy may be raised.

In Hanafi usul, not all causes in qiyas are equal. Some are merely descriptive (tard) and can be challenged by inconsistency. But when a cause has been confirmed as effective by a textual source, it gains a special immunity. This lesson explains that immunity and the one valid objection that remains.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish descriptive from effective causes
Understand that descriptive causes are derived by observation and can be invalidated by naqd, while effective causes are established by a revealed source (Quran, Sunnah, or Ijma) and are immune.
Descriptive causes are called 'tard' in Hanafi terminology.
2
Identify the source of effectiveness
Recognize that an effective cause must have its effect shown by the source in at least one instance. Examples: Quran 4:43 for exit from privy causing hadath, hadith of cat for purity of leftover, Ijma for not cutting hand on third theft.
The cause is not derived by mere observation but by textual indication.
3
Understand the immunity
Once effective, the cause cannot be invalidated by inconsistency (al-munaqadah) or invalidity of basis (fasd al-wad‘). The opponent cannot say 'the cause is invalid because the ruling is absent in some case'.
Immunity is analogous to the immunity of the sources themselves.
4
Know the only permissible objection
The only objection allowed is counter-analogy (al-mu‘aradah), where the opponent provides a stronger cause or shows the cause is overridden by a stronger text or principle.
This is a narrow exception, not a general rebuttal.

بالمعنى الثابت بالوصف ثم بالحكم ثم بالغرض والتفصيل يأتي انتظره. فبعض النقوض يدفع بالمعنى الثابت بالوصف، وبعضه بغيره من الأقسام الأربعة الخارج من غير السبيلين إلخ فالخارج النجس علة للحدث، وقد ثبت تأثيره مرةً في السبيلين بقوله تعالى: أَوْ جَاءَ أَحَدٌ مِنْكُمْ مِنَ الْغَائِطِ . عليه: أي على تعليلنا هذا بالنقض من قبل الشافعي له. إذا لم يسل : أي لم يتجاوز المخرج فإنه نجس خارج وليس بحدث، فقد تخلف الحكم الحدث هذه العلة وهي الخارج؛ لأن تحت كل جلدة رطوبة وفي كل عرق دماً. ظاهراً لا خارجاً فالحاصل أن الوصف الذي هو علة للحدث ليس بموجود في مادة التخلّف، فإن العلة هي الخارج النجس، وما لم يسل ليس بخارج بل هو باد. ثم بالمعنى الثابت إلخ أي ثم ندفعه ثانياً بعدم المعنى الثابت بالوصف الذي كان ثبوته بالوصف بالدلالة، وبسبب هذا المعنى كان ذلك الوصف علة للحكم، فإذا لم يوجد فيه ذلك المعنى لم يكن علة، فإذا لم يوجد العلة لم يتخلّف الحكم كما تقول في ذلك المثال لو سلّم أن وصف الخروج موجود لكنه لم يوجد فيه المعنى الذي كان بسبه علة. وهو وجوب إلخ: لأنه يزيل بخروج النجس الطهارة الحاصلة للبدن ،كله، فيجب أولاً غسل ذلك الموضع من النجاسة ثم غسل البدن كله، ولكن لما كان فيه حرج عظيم اقتصر على الأعضاء الأربعة دفعاً للحرج فيه: أي بسبب وجوب غسل ذلك الموضع. ما يكون منه أي ما يخرج منه، لا ما يكون من خارج، فإن النجاسة الخارجية يوجب غسل ذلك الموضع فقط. لا يحتمل إلخ: فلما وجب غسل ذلك الموضع وجب غسل كل البدن لا محالة لعدم العلة: فكأنه لم يوجد الخروج لعدم المعنى المذكور، فلم يوجد الحكم وهو الحدث، لا أن العلة موجود والحكم متخلف كما قال الخصم.
Chapter of Qiyas. The effective causes, countering, and inconsistency. These aspects force the proponents of descriptive attributes to claim effect. As for effective causes, the questioner has after countering no recourse except counter-analogy; because effective causes do not admit inconsistency and invalidity of basis after their effect has been shown by the Book, Sunnah, or Ijma. But if an inconsistency is imagined, it must be repelled in four ways. After the exit of impurity for the removal of purity: for the entire body becomes impure by the exit of any impurity, so this is intelligible. Thus the analogy would be to wash the entire body after the exit of impurity. For impurity that exits less, the ruling remains on analogy like semen. But for that which exits more like urine, it was limited to washing the four limbs which are the principles of the body in the boundaries and the occurrence of sins, to remove hardship; because washing the whole body each time is a great hardship. So restricting to the four limbs is not rationally based, and wudu is not such restriction that it be considered irrational requiring intention. As for the impurity of the body by the exit of impurity and its removal by water, it is a rational matter not requiring intention, unlike tayammum, for soil is itself contaminating, not purifying by nature, hence it requires intention. The four ways (countering, counter-analogy, invalidity of basis, inconsistency) are brought against descriptive causes. From it, except counter-analogy, it is indicated that countering applies to it, and what precedes it is the statement of the requisite of the cause, and what follows countering does not apply: namely invalidity of basis, counter-analogy, and inconsistency. For the Sunnah or Ijma: just as the Book, Sunnah, and Ijma do not admit inconsistency and invalidity of basis, likewise the causes established by each of the Book, Sunnah, and Ijma do not admit them. Example of what shows its effect by the Book: what exits from the body, for it is a cause of hadath, its effect appeared once in the two passages by His saying: 'Or if one of you comes from the privy' (4:43). Example of what shows its effect by the Sunnah: circumambulation, for it is a cause for the non-impurity of the leftover of domestic animals, its effect appeared once in the leftover of the cat by his saying: 'It is from those who go around you and go around' (Tirmidhi 92). Example of what shows its effect by Ijma: destruction, for it is a cause for not cutting the hand of the thief in the third instance, for it involves losing the full benefit, and the punishment for theft was prescribed as a deterrent, not destructive by Ijma. So if his hand were cut in the third instance, destruction prohibited by Ijma would occur. Those causes do not admit invalidity of basis at all as not being fit as a cause, because their causation appeared once from the Book, Sunnah, and Ijma. As for inconsistency, it applies to them in form though not in reality. And to that he referred with his saying 'But if it is imagined, etc.': it is repelling by the description then. Chapter of Qiyas. Effective causes, countering, and inconsistency. Just as we say regarding what exits from other than the two passages: it is impure exiting from a human, so it is hadath like urine. Then it is objected upon with the case when it does not flow. So we repel it first by the description: it is not an exit; because under every skin there is moisture, and in every vein blood. When the skin is removed, it becomes apparent (i.e., it leaves its place, blood and the like become apparent), not an exit. Then by the meaning established by the description by indication, which is the obligation to wash that spot for purification. In that the description becomes a proof in that the obligation of purification in the body is considered based on what comes from it (the impure exit). What comes from it does not admit division of the description, and there the washing of that spot was not obligatory, so the ruling is absent in other than those cases due to absence of the cause. By the meaning established by the description, then by the ruling, then by the purpose; the detail will come, so wait. Some inconsistencies are repelled by the meaning established by the description, and some by other of the four divisions: what exits from other than the two passages, etc. So the impure exit is a cause for hadath, and its effect has been established once in the two passages by His saying: 'Or if one of you comes from the privy'. Upon it: i.e., upon our causation this by inconsistency from al-Shafi'i. 'If it does not flow': i.e., does not exceed the exit, then it is impure exiting but not hadath, so the ruling of hadath is absent from this cause which is the exit. Because under every skin there is moisture, and in every vein blood. Apparent, not exiting. So the conclusion is that the description which is the cause for hadath is not present in the case where it is absent. For the cause is the impure exit, and what does not flow is not an exit but rather apparent. Then by the meaning established etc.: i.e., then we repel it secondly by the absence of the meaning established by the description by indication, and because of this meaning that description was a cause for the ruling. So if that meaning is not present in it, it is not a cause. So if the cause is not present, the ruling is not absent, as you say in that example: even if the description of exit is conceded, the meaning due to which it was a cause is absent. And that is the obligation etc.: because it removes by the exit of impurity the purity of the entire body, so first it is obligatory to wash that spot from impurity, then to wash the entire body. But because there is great hardship, it was limited to the four limbs to remove hardship. In it: i.e., due to the obligation to wash that spot. 'What comes from it': i.e., what exits from it, not what comes from outside, for external impurity requires washing only that spot. Does not admit etc.: so when washing that spot became obligatory, washing the entire body became inevitable, so it is as if the exit did not occur due to the absence of the mentioned meaning. So the ruling, which is hadath, is absent, not that the cause is present and the ruling is absent as the opponent says.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage discusses the defense of effective causes (al-‘ilal al-mu’aththira) in qiyas. The author distinguishes between descriptive causes and effective causes. Effective causes are those that have been proven to have an effect by the Quran, Sunnah, or Ijma. Once an effective cause is established by one of these sources, it cannot be invalidated by inconsistency (naqd) or invalidity of basis (fasd al-wad‘); the only permissible objection is counter-analogy (mu‘arada). However, if an inconsistency is only imagined in form, it must be repelled by one of four methods: repelling by the description (daf‘ bi al-wasf), by the meaning established by the description (bi al-ma‘na al-thabit bi al-wasf), by the ruling (bi al-hukm), or by the purpose (bi al-gharad). The text provides examples: for the cause of exiting from the body as a cause of hadath, it was proven effective by the Quran for the two passages. For the cat's saliva, the Prophet's hadith established that cats are from those who go around, so their leftover is pure. For the thief's hand, Ijma established that destruction is a cause for not cutting in the third instance. Then the text addresses an objection from al-Shafi‘i regarding something exiting from a wound that does not flow. The Hanafi response uses the first two methods: first, by the description, the thing is not considered an exit because it remains under the skin; second, by the meaning, the intended meaning (obligation to wash the spot) is absent when the exit does not flow, so the cause (exit) does not exist. Thus, there is no genuine inconsistency.

Key Terms
العلة المؤثرة (al-‘illah al-mu’aththirah): An effective cause whose legal effect has been confirmed by a textual source (Quran, Sunnah, or Ijma) in at least one instance.
المناقضة (al-munaqadah): An inconsistency where the cause is present but the ruling is absent, or vice versa, used by opponents to invalidate a cause.
الممانعة (al-mumana‘ah): Countering: the opponent argues that the alleged cause is not effective by presenting a case where the cause exists but the ruling does not, or vice versa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul discussed, what is the only acceptable objection against an effective cause (al-‘illah al-mu’aththira) that has been proven by a textual source?
A) Invalidity of basis (fasd al-wad‘)
B) Inconsistency (al-munaqadah)
C) Counter-analogy (al-mu‘aradah)
D) Countering (al-mumana‘ah)

Key Takeaway
Effective causes proven by Quran, Sunnah, or Ijma are immune to invalidation by inconsistency or invalid basis; only counter-analogy is permissible.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
10 min
Defending an Effective Cause: The Four Methods of Repelling Inconsistency
Applies the four methods to defend the cause of hadath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the four methods (daf‘ bi al-wasf, bi al-ma‘na, bi al-hukm, bi al-gharad) to defend an effective cause from an apparent inconsistency.

Core Idea
When an effective cause is challenged by an apparent inconsistency, it can be defended by denying the description, the intended meaning, the ruling, or the purpose.

In the previous lesson, you learned that effective causes are immune to outright invalidation. But what if an inconsistency appears in form? The Hanafi school has developed four methods to repel such apparent inconsistencies. This lesson walks through these methods using the classic example of impurity exiting from a wound that does not flow—a case where al-Shafi'i objected and the Hanafis responded.

Guided Path
1
Understand the apparent inconsistency
Recognize the scenario: impurity exits from a wound (not the two passages) but does not flow. Shafi'i says it breaks wudu because it is an impure exit; Hanafis say it does not. The cause (impure exit) appears to be present but the ruling (hadath) is absent.
This is called an apparent naqd (inconsistency) that must be repelled.
2
Apply the first method: daf‘ bi al-wasf
Deny that the description 'exit' is fully present. The thing is not 'exiting' because it remains under the skin. Thus the cause is not actually present, so there is no inconsistency.
The description must be genuinely present for the cause to operate.
3
Apply the second method: daf‘ bi al-ma‘na al-thabit bi al-wasf
Even if we concede the exit, the intended meaning of the cause (obligation to wash the entire body) is absent due to hardship. The ruling of hadath was lifted by istihsan. So the cause is not effective in this case because the meaning is absent.
The meaning is what makes the description a cause; if absent, the cause is considered non-existent.
4
Note the third and fourth methods
The third method (by the ruling) and fourth (by the purpose) are mentioned but not applied in this example. They are used when the first two fail. They involve showing that the ruling or purpose of the cause does not apply in the challenging case.
The text says 'some inconsistencies are repelled by the meaning, and some by other of the four divisions'.
